Tue  Stare  or  ALABAMA,  Montgomery  County

Be  it remembered  that  at  a  term  of  the  City  Court  of  Montgomery,
begun  and  holden  for  said  county,  at  the  Court  House  in  the  City
of  Montgomery,  State  and  County  aforesaid,  on  the  First  Monday  in
February  in  the  year  of  our  Lord  One  Thousand  Nine  Hundred  and
Bight,  it being  the  third  (3rd)  day  of said  month—present  the  Tion-
orable  W.  TL.  Thomas,  Associate  Judge  Presiding  in  the  Criminal
Division  of  said  Court—the  following  proceedings  were  had  and  the
following  judgments  were  rendered,  to-wit:

kv  Parte  Awoxzo  BAILEY.

To  Hon.  W.  HL.  Thomas,  Judge  of  the  City  Court  of  Montgomery :

Your  petitioner,  Alonzo

Bailey,  respectfully  shows  unto  your
Honor  that  he  is imprisoned  in  the  jail  of  Montgomery  County,  Ala-
bama,  and  the  cause  or  pretense  of  such  imprisonment  is  a  commiit-
ment  issued  by  B.C.  Young,  Esq.,  Justice  of  Peace  of  Montgomery
County,  Alabama,  a  copy  of  which  commitnent  is  hereunto  at-
tached,  marked  Exhibit  A,  and  made  a  part  of  this  petition  as  fully
as  if  set  out  herein.

The  premises  considered,  your  petitioner  prays  that  your  Honor
issue  a  writ  of  habeas  corpus  to  the  said  Horace  Hood  commanding
him  to  bring  the  body  of your  petitioner  before  your  Honor  at  a  time
and  place  to  be  fixed  by  your  Honor,  together  with  the  cause  of  de-
tention  of  petitioner,  and  that  upon  a  hearing  of  said  petition,  your
Honor  will  make  an  order  discharging  your  petitioner  from:  the  cus-
tody  of  said  Horace  Hood,

ALONZO  BAILEY,

Sworn  to  and  subseribed  before  me  this

Hlth  day  of  April,  1908,
J.  T.  LETCHER,

Notary  Public,  Montgomery  County,  Alabama,

Thereupon  Alonzo  Bailey,  by  his  attorneys,  made  the  following

motion :

entitled

to  amend  Section

Ev  Parte  Avoxzo  BAILEY.
Comes  Monzo  Bailey,  by  his  attorneys,  and  moves  the  Ton,  WH.
Thomas,  to  discharge  hind  from  the  custody  of  Ton,  Horace  Ilood,
Sheriff  of  Montgomery  County,  \labama,  on  the  following  grounds,
towit:
1.  The  evidence  introduced  fails  to  prove  an  intent  to  injure  or
defraud  the  Riverside  Co,  at  the  thie  of  entering  into  the  Contract.
»  The  evidence  fails  to  show  an  intent  to  tajure  or  defraud  the
Riverside  Co,  at  the  time  of  the  refusal  or  failure  to  perform  the  serv-
ices  Contracted  for.
47300  of  the
Criminal  Code  of  1896  approved  October  1,  1908,  ax  amended
by  an  aet  entitled  an  aet  to  amend  an  act  to  amend  Section
7
4730  of the  Criminal  Code  of  TS95,  approved  October  1,  1905;
approved  Aug.  15,  1907,  is  unconstitutional  and  void  in  that  it: pro-
vides  that  the  refusal  or  failure  of  euy  person  who  enters  inte  such
contract,  ete.  shall  be  prima  facie  evidence  of  ihe  intent  to  injure
or  defraud  his  employer,  and  the  effect  of such  provision  ix to  deprive
defendant  of  his  liberty  without  due  process  of  law  in  violation  of
Section  6 of  the  Constitution  of  the  State  of  Mabama.
4.  Said  act  is  unconstitutional  and  void  in  that  it  violates  that
part  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the  Constitution  of  the  United
States  which  provides  that  no  state  shall  make  or  enforce  any  law
whieh  will  deprive  any  person  of  life,  Tiberty  or  property  without
due  process  of  law.
6.  Said  act  is uncoustitutional  and  void  in  that  it violates  that  part
of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the  —  United  States  which  pro-
vides  that  no  state  shall  deny  to  any  person  within  its  jurisdiction
the  equal  protection  of  the  laws.
>  Said  vet  is unconstitutional  and  veid  in  that  its  effect  is to  sub-
ject  the  defendant  to  involuntary  servitude  not  as  a  punishment  for
any  crime,  in  violation  of  the  Thirteenth  Amendment  of  the  Con-
stitution  of  the  United  States.
S  Said  act  makes  the  failure  or  refusal  to  perform:  the  services
under  the  contract  prima  facie  evidence  of  only  one  intent.
Q  Beeause  it  cannot  be  determined  from  said  act  whether  the
failure  or  refusal  to  perform  services  is  prima  facie  evidence  of  the
intent  at  the  time  of  entering  inte  the  contract  or  at  the  time  of  the
failure  or  refusal  to  perform:  the  services,

WATTS  & LETCHER,

Attarneys  for  Petitioner.

ALONZO  BAILEY  VS.  THE  STATE  OF  ALABAMA.

The  insistence  was  overturned  and  the  statute  was  held  not  to  be  un-
constitutional—the  Court,  as  the  basis  of  the  ruling,  again  pointing
out  the  fact  that  “a  mere  breach  of contract  is  not  by the  statute  made
acrime,”  but  that  the  criminal  feature  consists  in  the  intent  to  injure
or  defraud.

This  fntent  to  injure  or  de frand  tnarks  the  line  of  cleavage  be-
tween  the  statute  in  judgment  and  the  one  approved  Mareh  ist,  1901
(Acts,  1900-1,  p.  1208).  which  made  it  a  misdemeanor  for  any  per-
son  who  had  contracted  in  writing  to  labor  for,  or  serve,  another  for
any  given  time,  ete.,  and  who,  before  the  expiration  of  such  contract,
and  without  the  consent  of  the  other  party,  and  without  suflicient  ex-
cuse  (to  be  judged  by  the  court),  shall  leave  such  other  party,  ete.
This  last  statute  was,  by  Judge  Jones,  of  the  Federal  Court,  held  to
be  obnoxious  to  the  State  Constitution  (123  Fed.  Rep.  671):  and  was
by  this  Court  held  to  be  unconstitutional,  in  Toney’s  Case
(141  Ala.  120),  because  of  the  restrictions  it  attempts  to  place
These  two  cases  are  now
on  the  right  to  make  contracts.
urged  as  authority  in  support  of  the  insistence  of  appellant  that  the
statute  under  consideration  is  violative  of  the  Constitution,  and  we
are  asked  to  overrule  the  Vann  Case,  supra.

12

While  it  is  clear  that  a  mere  breach  of  contract  cannot  be  made
the  foundation  for  a  criminal  offense.  and  that  undue  restrictions
cannot  be  placed  on  the  right  of  an  individual  to  enter  Into  contracts,
yet  when  the  individual  enters  into  a  contract,  with  the  intention  to
perpetrate  a  fraud,  it  is equally  obvious  that  he  passes  over  the  con-
stitutional  boundary  line  in  respect  to  the  free  right  to  contract;  and
it  is within  legislative  competency  to  enact  a  law  penalizing  the  enter-
ing  into  a  contract,  with  such  intent,  and  obtaining  money  or  other
personal  property  through  such  agency.

Giana
RUN

SD zee
SLEW

This  is  all  that  is  effectuated  by  the  legislation  in  question.

On
its  face,  the  purpose  is  to  punish  fraudulent  practices,  not  the  mere
failure  to  pay  a  debt.

Thus  considered,  it  is  constitutional.

Without  further  extension  of  the  argument,  we  not  only  decline
to  depart  from  the  ruling  made  in  Vani’s  Case,  on  this  subject,  but
reaffirm  it.—PBanks  v.  State,  2.1.  Ro  AL  ONL  S.),  1007,

In  Ev  parte  Riley  (OA  Nla.  $2.85)  it  was  said:  As  the  intent  is
the  design,  purpose,  resolve  or  determination  in  the  mind  of  the  ac-
cused,  it  can  rarely  be  proven  by  direct  evidence,  but  must  be  ascer-
tained  by  means  of  inferences  from:  the  facts  and  circumstances  de-
In  the  absence,  however,  of  evidence  from
veloped  by  the  proof.
which  such  inferences  may  be  drawn,  the  jury  are  not  justified  in
indulging  im  mere  unsupported  conjectures,  speculations  or  suspi-
cions  as  to  the  intentions  which  were  not  disclosed  by  any  visible  or
tangible  act,  expression  or  circumstance.”

It  is  no  doubt  true  that  the  difficulty  i  proving  the  intent,  made
patent  by  that  decision,  suggested  the  amendment  of  1903,  to  the
statute,  which  provides  that  the  refi  al  or  failure  of  a  person  who
enters  into  such  contract,  to  perforta  such  act  or  service,  or  refund
such  money,  or  pay  for  such  property,  without  just  cause,  shall  be
prima  facie  evidence  ef  the  intent  to  injure  or  defraud  his  emplover.
This  amendment  has  twice  been  declared  by  this  Court  to  be  a

net at th a ah

Porm

constitutional  enactment.—Thomas’s  Case,

assailed,  and  the  conclusions  reached  therein  are  vigorously
mnhated  in  brief  of  appellant's  counsel.

Order  Granting  Writ  of Levror,

On  this  the  [Sth  day  of  August,  1908,  came  the  petitioner-appel-
lant,  by  his  attorney,  in  due  time,  and  filed  herein,  and  presented  to
the  court  his  petition,  praying  for  the  allowance  of  a  writ  of  error
intended  to  be  urged  by  him,  praying  also  that  a  transcript  of  the
record  and  proceedings  and  papers  upon  which  the  judgment  herein
was  rendered,  duly  authenticated,  may  be  sent  to  the  Honorable,  the
Supreme  Court  of  the  United  States,  and  that  such  other  and  further
proceedings  inay  be  had  as  may  be  proper  in  the  premises,
On  consideration  whereof,  the  security  being  now  offered  by  peti-
tioner-appellant,  and  approved,  the  said  petition  has  this  day  been
duly  allowed,  and  it  is,  therefore,  ordered  that  upon  the  filing  of  said
bond  with  the  clerk  of this  court  in  the  sum  of seven  hundred  dollars,
it  shall  operate  as  a  supersedeas,  and  all  other  and  further  proceed-
ings  in  this  court  be  and  they  are  hereby  suspended  and  stayed  until
the  determination  of  said  writ  of  error  by  the  said  Supreme  Court  of
the  United  Stites.

fraud  the  Riverside  Co.  at  the  time  of  the  refusal  or  failure  to  per-
form  the  services  contracted  for,

¢p.  4.)

The  statute  upon  which  the  Court  acted  was  Section

4730  of  the  Code  of  Alabama,  (1896)  as  amended.

The  said  section  before  the  amendment  was  as  follows:

Deena
Fiat
ae inntades

yt RIN

Tae

=...

lian

“4730  (3812).  SAME:  UNDER  CONTRACT
FOR  PERFORMANCE  OF  ACT  OR  SERVICE.—
Any  person,  who,  with  intent  to  injure  or  defraud
his  employer,  enters  into  a  contract  in  writing  for
the  performance  of  any  act  or  service,  and  thereby
obtains  money  or  other  personal  property  from  such
employer,  and,  with  like  intent,  and  without  just
cause,  and  without  refunding  such  money,  or  pay-
ing  for  such  property,  refuses  to  perform  such  act
or  service,  must,  on  conviction,  be  punished  as  if he
had  stolen  it.”

The  said  Section  4730  was  amended  by  the  General

Acts  of  1903,  page  345,  so  as  to  read  as  follows:

“Section  4730.  Any  person,  who,  with  intent  to
injure  or  defraud  his  employer,  enters  into  a  con-
tract  in  writing  for  the  performance  of  any  act  or
service;  and  thereby  obtains  money  or  other  person-
al  property  from  such  employer,  and  with  like  in-
tent,  and  without  just  cause,  and  without  refund-
ing  such  money,  or  paying  for  such  property,  re-
fuses  or  fails  to  perform  such  act  or  service,  must
on  conviction  be  punished  as  if  he  had  stolen  it.
And  the  refusal  or  failure  of any  person,  who  enters
into  such  contract,  to  perform  such  act  or  service,
or  refund  such  money,  or  pay  for  such  property,
without  just  cause,  shall  be  prima  facie  evidence  of
the  intent  to  injure  or  defraud  his  employer.”

The  said  amended  statute  was  further  amended  by the
General  Acts  of  1907,  page  636,  so  as  to  read  as  follows:

“Section  4730.  Any  person,  who,  with  intend  to
injure  or  defraud  his  employer,  enters  into  a  con-
tract  in  writing  for  the  performance  of  any  act  or

service,  and  thereby  obtains  money  or  other  personal
property  from  such  employer,  and  with  like  intent,
and  without  just  cause,  and  without  refunding
such  money,  or  paying  for  such  property,  refuses
or  fails  to  perform  such  act  or  service,  must  on  con-
viction  be  punished  by a  fine  in  double  the  damage
suffered  by  the  injured  party,  but  not  more  than
$300.00,  one-half  of said  fine  to  go  to  the  county  and
one-half  to  the  party  injured;  and  any  person,  who,
with  intent  to  injure  or  defraud  his  landlord,  enters
into  any  contract  in  writing  for  the  rent  of  land,
and  thereby  obtains  money  or  other  personal  prop-

in  double  the  damage  suffered  by the  injured  party,
but  not  more  than  $300.00,  one-half  of said  fine  to
go  to  the  county  and  one-half  to  the  injured  party.
And  the  refusal  or  failure  of  any  person,  who  en-
ters  into  such  contract,  to  perform  such  act  or  ser-
vice  or  to cultivate  such  land,  or  refund  such  money,
or  pay  for  such  property  without  just  cause  shall
be  prima  facie  evidence  of  the  intent  to  injure  his
employer  or  landlord  or  to  defraud  him.

The  said  Supreme  Court  of  Alabama  erred  in  af-

firming  the  action  of  the  court  below.

The  said  Supreme  Court  of Alabama  erred  in  not
reversing  the  action  of  the  court  below  and  directing  the
discharge  of  the  plaintiff  in  error.

The  act  entitled  an  act  to  amend  Seec-

Fifth  (6).

That  the  said  act  is  unconstitutional  and
void  in  that  it  violates  that  part  of  the  Fourteenth

which  provides  that  no  state  shall  deny  to  any  person
Within  its  jurisdiction  the  equal  protection  of  the  laws.

IBS

6

Sixth  (7).  That  said  act  is  unconstitutional  and  void
in  that  its  effect  is to  subject  the  plaintiff  to  involuntary
servitude  not  as  a  punishment  for  a  crime,  in  violation
of  the  Thirteenth  amendment  of  the  Constitution  of  the
United  States.

(Record,  p.  14).

INTRODUCTION,

This  case  presents  a  question  of  liberty;  a  question
which  involves  directly  or  indirectly  the  liberty  or  rights
of  thousands  of  citizens.
It  is  a  question  involving
specifically  the  liberty  of  Alonzo  Bailey,  a  negro  la-
borer.
It  involves  the  constitutionality  of  said  Section
4730  as  finally  amended.

This  court  has  always  carefully

cases
scrutinized
where  the  Constitution  is  involved.
It  has  held  that  in
doubtful  cases  it  would  not  declare  an  act  of  a  State
Legislature  contrary  to  the  Constitution,  yet
it  has
never  hesitated  to  do  so  when  the  statute  deprived  one
of  his  liberty  without  “due  process  of  law,”  or  “equal
protection  of  the  laws”  or  enforced  involuntary  servi-
tude  except  in  punishment  of  crime.

The  purpose  and  effect  of  the  first  section  of  the  Four-
teenth  Amendment  to  the  Constitution  of  the  United
States,  as  stated  in  United  States  vs.  Harris,  106  U.  8S.
629,  was  clearly  defined  by  Mr.  Justice  Bradley  in  the
case  of  United  States  vs.  Cruikshank,  1  Wood  316,  as
follows:

i

“It  isa  guaranty  of  protection  against  the  acts  of
the  State  Government  itself.
/t  is  @  guaranty
against  the  exertion  of  arbitrary  and  tyrannical
power  on  the  part  of  the  government  and  legis-
lature  of  a  state.”  It  furnishes  an  “additional
guaranty  against  any  encroachment  by  the  State

gt  atone A hb oP  AG  ARIK  POPE.

APES)  PS  ITR.

°
‘

upon  the  fundamental  rights  which  belong  to  every
citizen  as  a  member  of  society.”

United  States  vs.  Cruikshank,  92  U.  S.  542.
United  States  vs.  Lee,  106  U.S.  196.

“DUE  PROCESS  OF  LAW.”

The  amended  act  is  unconstitutional  and  void  in  that
it  violates  that  part  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of
the  Constitution  of  the  United  States  which  provides
that  no  state  shall  make  or  enforce  any  law  which  shall
deprive  any  person  of  life,  liberty  or  property  without
“due  process  of  law.”

On  account  of  a  rule  of  evidence  in  Alabama  which
prevents  a  person  from  testifying  as  to  his  uncommuni-
cated  motives,  purpose  or  intent,  plaintiff  in  error  was
unable  to  show  by  his  own  testimony  that  he  entered
into  the  contract  in  good  faith  and  that  he  rescinded  the
contract  for  reasons  other  than  those  constituting  a
crime.

In  McCormick  vs.  Joseph,  77  Ala.  236-240  it  is said:

“It  is  well  settled  in  this  State,  whatever  the  rule
nay  be  elsewhere,  that  witnesses  are  not  permitted
to  testify  to  their  motive,  belief  or  intention,  when

secret  or  uncommunicated ; such  mental  status  when
relevant,  being  a  matter  of  inference  to  be  determ-
ined  from  the  circumstances  of  the  case  by  the
jury.”

Holmes  ys.  State,  136  Ala.  80-84.
Johnson  vs.  State,  102  Ala.  1-16.
Brown  vs.  State,  79  Ala.  51-54.
Harrison  vs.  State,  78  Ala.  5-11.
Alexander  vs.  Alexandei,  71  Ala.  295-8.
Burns  vs.

Campbell,  71  Ala.  271-291.

ay

eine

-  SPN EN nL Ee  NNSA  PRISER. T  ENE TRAIL ILO  ENE  OTDM

I  te  eR

8

Burke  vs.  State,  71  Ala.  3877-382.
Wheeless  vs.  Rhodes,  70  Ala.  419-421.

There  was  no  witness  to  the  execution  of  the  contract
by  whom  plaintiff  in  error  could  prove  his  good  faith,
nor  was  there  any  witness  to  the  rescission  of  the  said
contract  by  whom  he  could  prove  that  he  terminated  the
contract  fer  reasons  other  than  those  constituting  a
crime;  the  only  witness  being  the  General  Manager  of
the  Company,  with  whom  the  contract  was  made.  From
the  very  nature  of  things,  no  evidence  could  be  adduced
on  the  question  of  intent.  Any  declaration  made  by  him
as  to  his  motives  either  in  entering  into  the  contract  or
in  failing  or  refusing  to  perform  said  contract  was  inad-
It  has
missible  as  evidence  in  his  favor  on  the  trial.
been  established  in  Alabama  that  a  defendant  cannot
thus  make  evidenec  for  himself.

Harkness  vs.  State,  129  Ala.  71.

In  the  case  at  bar,  the  proof  of  the  execution  of  the
contract  and  plaintiff's  failure  or  refusal  to  perform  the
said  contract  operated  by  the  terms  of  the  statute  to
But
make  the  petitioner  prima  facie  guilty  of  a  crime.
in  as  much  as  the  right  of  rebuttal  is  denied  under  the
law,  as  above  explained,  the  statute  prescribes  a  rule  of
evidence  that  is  in  reality  conclusive.

It  is  conceded  by  the  weight  of  authority,  that  if  the
legislature  prescribed  what  is  conclusive  evidence  or  if
it  takes  away  from  a  defendant  the  fair  opportunity  to
establish  his  case  of defense,  the  act  of the  legislature  is
contrary  to  that  part  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of
the  Constitution  of the  United  States  which  insures  “due
process  of  law,”  and  is  therefore  unconstitutional  and
void.

Harvey  vs.  Elliot,  167  U.  S.  409.
3x  parte  Well,  107  U.S.  289.

hE  RE  ETT

9

ml
+0 ba

Gaplin  vs.  Page,  85  U.  8.  350.
Darthmouth  College  vs.  Woodward,  4  Wheaton

518.

The  legislature  cannot  under  the  guise  of  enacting  a
prima  facie  rule  of  evidence,  fix  a  conclusive  one.
It  is
unimportant  that  the  legislature  calls  such  a  rule  prima
facie.
If  it operates  as  a  conclusive  rule  it  is as  baneful
in  its  effect  as  if it  was  so  called.
In  either  event,  it  is
contrary  to  the  constitutional  guaranty  of  “due  process
of law.”  That  the  statute  in  question  is offensive  to  this
provision  of  the  Constittuion  cannot  be  doubted.

In  the  case  of  Dartmouth  College  vs.  Woodward,  4
Wheaton  U.  S.  518-581,  which  has  been  relied  upon,
probably  more  than  any  other  authority,  to  throw  light
upon  and  to  explain  the  rights,  protection  and  franchises
deprived  from  the  grant  of  the  Government,  Mr.  Daniel
Webster,  in  his  argument,  quoted  from  Lord  Cope’s  Ex-
planation  of  the  29th  Chap.  of  Magna  Charta:

“*No  man  shall  be  disseized,  ete.,  unless  it  be  by
the  lawful  judgment,  that  is,  verdict  of  equals,  or
by  the  law  of  the  land,  that  is  (to  speak  it once  for
all),  by the  due  course  and  process  of law?  ”,

AF TL -

He  then  said:

“By  the  law  of  the  land  is  most  clearly  intended
the  general  law;  a  law  which  hears  before  it  con-
demns;  which  proceeds  upon  inquiry,  and  renders
judgment  only  after  trial.
The  meaning  is,  that
every  citizen  shall  hold  his  life,  liberty,  property
and  immunities,  under  the  protection  of  the  general
rules  which  govern  society.  Everything  which  may
pass  under  the  form  of an  enactment,  is  not,  there-
fore,  to  be  considered  the  law  of the  land”,
*

*

*

10

“Such  a  strange  construction  would  render  consti-
tutional  provisions  of  the  highest  importance  com-
It  would  tend  direct-
pletely  inoperative  and  void.
ly to  establish  the  union  of  all  powers  in  the  legis-
lature.  There  would  be  no  general  permanent  law
for  courts  to  administer,  or  for  men  to  live  under.
The  administration  of  justice  would  be  an  empty
Judges  would  sit  to  exe-
form,  an  idle  ceremony.
cute  legislative  judgments  and  decrees;  not  to  de-
clare  the  law,  or  to  administer  the  justice  of  the
country.”

In  Wright  vs.  © adjebaugh,  3  L.  Ed.  541,  it  was  held
that  “due  process  of  law”  requires  that  a  party  shall  be
properly  brought  into  court,  and  when  there,  shall  have
a  right  to  set  up  any  lawful  defense,
In  Hubey  vs.  Reily,  58  Pa.  (3  P.  F.  Smith)  112,  it
was  held  that  “due  process  of law”  includes  an  opportun-
ity  to  answer.
Clerk  ys.  Mitchell,  64  Mo.  564,  said  “due  process  of

law”  means  a  law  which  hears  before  it  condemns.
Both  as  a  matter  of  law  and  of  fact,  every  person  is
presumed  to  be  innocent  until  his  guilt  is proved  beyond
a  reasonable  doubt  and  it  is  the  high  and  solemn  duty
of  the  court  to  see  that  all  of  the  elements  of  the  crime
are  proved  or  that  testimony  is  adduced  which  justifies
the  jury  in  finding  these  elements.
Clyatt  vs.  United States,  197  UV.  8.  207-222.
The  legislature  further  denied  “due  process  of  law”
when  it  amended  said  statute  so  as  to  authorize  the
court  to  convict  of  fraud  on  no  other  evidence  than  that,
of  the  execution  of  a  contract,  the  failure  to  return  the
consideration  therefor,  in  case  of  its  breach  or  the  fail-
ure  to  perform  the  services  provided  for  in  the  said  con-

State  vs.  Beach,  147  Ind.  74.
Meadowcraft  vs.  People,  163  11.  56,

The  said  amended  statute  further  denies  “due  process
of  law”,  in  that,  there  is an  attempted  substitution  of the
judicial  department  of  the  State  government  for  the  leg-
islative.

To  raise  a  prima  facie  case  (which  almost  invariably
establishes  the  guilt  and  without  which  the  plaintiff  in
error  could  not  have  been  held)  it  must  be  shown  that
the  defendant  refused  or  failed  to  perform  the  contraet
“without  just  cause”.
What  constitutes  “just  cause”?
The  legislature,  contrary  to  its  duty,  has  not  defined  it.
No  attorney  can  advise  what  constitutes  it.  No  man  can
tell  whether  or  not  he  is  within  the  limits  of  the  law,
as  different  juries  might  reach  different  conclusions  un-
der  the  same  state  of  facts.  Whether  or  not  one  should
be  burdened  with  a  prima  facie  case  depends  upon  the
view  reached  by the  jury,  which  depends  upon  elements
which  no  man,  with  certainty,  can  foresee.
There  is  no
criterion  laid  down  by the  statute  by which  one  can  reg-
ulate  his  actions  so  as  to  know  that  he  is  abiding  by the
law.

In  United  States  v.  Reese,  92  U.  S.  214,  cited  with
approval  by  Mr.  Justice  Brewer  in  James  v.  Bowman,
190  U.  S.  127-140,  the  Court  said:

Judge  Thos.  G.  Jones,  in  Peonage  Cases,  128  Fed.
671,  held  the  act  of  the  Legislature  of  Alabama  of  Mar.
1, 1901,  General  Acts,  1900-1901,  page  125,  unconstitu-
tionel,  which  made  it a  penal  offense,  where  any  one  who
has  contracted  in  writing  to  labor  or  serve  another,  or
Who  has  leased  or  rented  lands  for  any  specified  time,
should  without  the  consent  of  the  other  party  and  with-
out  sufficient  excuse,  to  be  adjudged  by  the  eourt,  fail
to  carry  out  such  contract,  and  take  employment  of  a
similar  nature  without  giving  notice  of  the  prior  con-
tract.  And  on  page  688,  said:

“It  is  @  vicious  species  of  class  legislation.

Its
criminal  penalties  are  grafted  only  upon  contracts
between  laborers  and  renters  on  farm  lands,  and
their  hirers  or  landlords.
It  is  designed  solely  in
the  interest  of the  employer  or  landlord.  The  latter
may  discharge  the  laborer  or  renter,  or  fail  to  come

They  are  not  treated  as  other

conditions  and  with  like  protection,
Uf  litigation
terminates  adversely  to  them,  they  are  muleted  in
the  attorney's  fee  of  the  successful  plaintiff:  if  it
terminates  in  their  favor  they  recover  no  attorney’s
fees.
It  is no  sufficient  answer  to  say  that  they  are
punished  only  when  adjudged  to  be  in  the  wrong.
They  do  not  enter  the  courts  upon  equal  terms.
They  must  pay  attorney's  fees  if  wrong;  they  do
not  recover  any  if  right;  while  their  adversaries
recover  if right  and  pay  nothing  if wrong.

INVOLUNTARY  SERVITUDE,

it  is  further  submitted  to  the  Court  that  the  identical
result  is obtained  under  the  said  amended  statute  which
was  obtained  under  the  “System  of  Peonage.”

In  New  Mexico,  at  the  time  of  the  passage  of  the
Peonage  Statute  (Section  1990  and  5526  Revised  Stat-
utes)  there  existed  a  system  of  labor  inherited  from
The
Spain  which  was  commonly  known  as  “Peonage”,
system,  however,  was  not  known  as  Peonage  by  the  laws
of  New  Mexico  but  as  a  relation  of  master  and  servant.
Peouage  was  not  slavery  as  formerly  existed
in_  this
Country.  The  peon  was  a  free  man  possessing  both  civil
and  political  rights.  The  essentials  of  the  Peonage  con-
tract  were  consent  of  parties,  the  thing  or  service  con-
tracted  for,  the  duration  of  the  contract  and  the  consid-
After  entering  into  the  contract,  the
eration  therefor,
peou  became  bound  to  the  master  “for  an  indebtedness
founded  upon  an  advancement  in  consideration  of  ser-
The  Peonage  Contract  could  be  rescinded  only
vice.”
by  mutual  consent,  by  payment  of  the  indebtedness  or
on  account  of  some  grave  injustice.
If  the  peon  refused
to  fulfill  his  part  of  the  contract  “the  court  would  com-
pel  him  to  pay  the  principal  and  interest  to  the  other

vices  of  the  peon  to  the  highest  bidder,
The  same  pro-

pay  what  he  was  ordered.”

CONCLUSION,

It  is  respectfully  ufged  that  this  Court  cannot  escape
the  conclusion  that  the  said  amended  statute  is  repug-
nant  to  so  much  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the
Constitution  of  the  United  States  which  guarantees
“Due  process  of  law”  and  “Equal  protection  of  the
laws”  and  that  the  said  statute  is  equally  repugnant  to
the  Thirteenth  Amendment  of  the  Constitution,  and

ALONZO  BAILEY,  PLAINTIFF  IN  ERROR,
v.
THE  STaTvTeE  OF  ALABAMA.

( No.  538.
)

IN ERROR  TO  THE  SUPREME  COURT  OF  THE  STATE  OF

ALABAMA,

I.

THIS  STATUTE  IS  UNCONSTITUTIONAL  AND  VOID

BECAUSE  IT  VIOLATES  THE  THIRTEENTH  AMENDMENT

TO  THE  CONSTITUTION  OF  THE  UNITED  STATES.

To  enforce  the  thirteenth  amendment,  Congress
passed  the  act  carried  into  Revised  Statutes,  section
1990:

*

¥*

%*

*

all  acts,  laws,  resolutions,  orders,
regulations,  or  usages  of the  Territory  of New
Mexico  or  any  other  Territory  or  State,
*
*  by  virtue  of  which  any  attempt
shall  hereafter  be made  to  establish,  maintain,
or  enforce,  directly  or  indirectly,  the  voluntary
or  involuntary  service  or  labor  of  any  persons
as  peons  in  liquidation  of  any  debt  or  obliga-
tion,  or  otherwise,  are  declared  null  and  void.

61917—08—1

ee  ee cate en  eee OR  ae  ee

RY

2

In  Clyatt  v.  United  States  (197  U.8.,  207)  this

court  tersely  said:

That  which  is  contemplated  by  the  statute
is  compulsory  service  to  secure  the  payment
of a  debt.

The  thirteenth  amendment  operated  upon  the  indi-
vidual  and  determined  that  involuntary  servitude,  by
whatever  name  it  might  be  known  or  whatever  form
it  could  assume,  should  not  exist  anywhere  in  the
Republic.  And  the  statute,  in  the  most  comprehen-_
sive  terms,  declares  absolutely  void  any  attempt,  in
any  manner,  by  legislation  or  in  any  other  way,
directly  or  indirectly,  to  bring  into  being,  in  any
If the
degree,  a  condition  of  involuntary  servitude.
effect  of any  law,  State  or  Federal,  is to  produce  this
result,  such  law  is a violation  of the  great  declaration
of personal  liberty  embodied  in the  thirteenth  amend-
ment.

The  statute  of Alabama,  under  which  the  plaintiff
in error  is held,  is one  of a  series  of acts,  the  evident
purpose  of  which  is  to  secure  compulsory  service  in
payment  of  a  debt.  By  an  act,  approved  March  1,
1901,  it was  made a misdemeanor  for  any  person  who
has contracted  in writing  to  labor  for or  serve  another
for any  given  time,  to  leave  the  service  of said  person
before  the  expiration  of  such  contract  and  without
the  consent  of said  employer,  and  to  make  a  second
contract  of similar  nature  or  character  with  another
person,  without  giving  such  second  employer  notice

3

of  the  first  contract.
This  act  was  declared  by  the
State  Supreme  Court  unconstitutional,  “because  of
the  restrictions  it  purports  to  place  on  the  right  to
make  contracts  for  employment  and  concerning  the
use  and  cultivation  of  land.”
(Toney  v.  State,  141
Ala.,  120,  125.)

United  States  District  Judge  Jones,  in  an  opinion
(Peonage  cases,  123  Fed.  Rep.,  671,  691)  also  declared
this  law  unconstitutional  upon  the  further  ground
that  it denied  to  laborers  the  equal  protection  of  the
law  guaranteed  by  the  fourteenth  amendment.

Subsequently  a  statute  was  passed  which  became
a  part  of  the  Alabama  Code,  section  4730.
This
statute  originally  was  included  in  the  law  providing
punishment  for  obtaining  money,  etc.,  by  false
pretenses.

At  following  sessions  of the  legislature  this  act  was
amended  by certain  additions.
It  is to  be  observed
that  these  amendments  served  to render  more  specific
the application  of the  law  to a  certain  class  of tenants
of land  and  laborers  and  to  accentuate  the  character
of  the  legislation  as  defining  a  criminal  offense  and
providing  the  punishment  for  its  commission.
The  present  amended  act  is as  follows:

6845.  Contract  for  performance  of  act  or
service.  Any  person  who,  with  intent  to  in-
jure  or  defraud  his  employer,  enters  into  a
contract  in writing  for  the  performance  of any
act  or  service,  and  thereby  obtains  money  or
other  personal  property  from  such  employer,

|

4

and  with  like  intent,  and  without  just  cause,
and  without  refunding  such  money,  or  paying
for  such  property,  refuses  or  fails  to  perform
such  act  or  service,  must,  on  conviction,  be
punished  by  a  fine  in  double  the  damage
suffered  by  the  injured  party,  but  not  more
than  three  hundred  dollars,  one-half  of  said
fine  to  go  to  the  county  and  one-half  to  the
party  injured;  and  any  person  who,  with
intent  to  injure  or  defraud  his  landlord,  enters
into  any  contract  in  writing  for  the  rent  of
land,  and  thereby  obtains  any  money  or  other
personal  property  from  such  landlord,  and
with  like  intent,  without  just  cause,  and  with-
out  refunding  such  money,  or  paying  for  such
property,  refuses  or  fails  to  cultivate  such
land,  or  to  comply  with  his  contract  relative
thereto,  must,  on  conviction,  be  punished  by
a  fine  in  double  the  damage  suffered  by  the
injured  party,  but  not  more  than  three  hun-
dred  dollars,  one-half  of  said  fine  to  go  to  the
county,  and  one-half  to  the  party  injured.
And  the  refusal  of any  person  who  enters  into
such  contract  to  perform  such  act  or  service
or  to  cultivate  such  lands,  or  refund  such
money,  or  pay  for  such  property,  without  just
cause,  shall  be  prima  facie  evidence  of  the
intent  to  injure  his  employer  or  landlord  or
to  defraud  him.

The  constitutionality  of  section  4730  has  been
attacked  in  the  courts  of  the  State  in  several  cases,
In  Vann’s
but  the  statute  has  been  there  upheld.
case  (43  Southern  Rep.,  357)  it was  insisted  that  the

5

act  was  repugnant  to  the  section  of the  Bill  of Rights
of Alabama,  ‘“‘that  no  person  shall  be  imprisoned  for
Adopting  the  language  in  another  case,  the
debt.”
court  said:

The  ingredients  of  the  offense  are:  (1)  A
contract  in  writing  by  the  accused  for  the
performance  of  any  act  or  service;  (2)  an
intent  on  the  part  of  the  accused,  when  he
entered  into  the  contract,  to  injure  or  defraud
his  employer;  (3)  the  obtaining  by  the  ac-
cused  of  money  or  other  personal  property
from  such  employer  by  means  of  such  con-
tract  entered  into  with  such  intent;  and  (4)
the  refusal  by  the  accused,  with  like  intent,
and  without  just  cause,  and  without  refund-
ing  such  money,  or  paying  for  such  property,
*  A
to  perform  such  act  or  service.
mere  breach  of  the  contract  is  not  made  a
The  criminal  feature  of  the  transac-
crime.
tion  is  wanting  unless  the  accused  entered
into  the  contract  with  intent  to  injure  or
defraud  his  employer,  and  unless  his  refusal
to  perform  was  with  like  intent  and  without
just  cause.

*

*

And  the  court  added:

While  the  statute  purges  the  offense  if  the
money  is repaid,  it does  not  convert  the  intent
on  the  part  of  the  legislature  into  seeking  im-
prisonment  for  debt,  rather  than  preventing
The  statute  could  be  a
and  punishing  fraud.
valid  one  for  punishment  of  fraud  with  this
requirement  or  proviso  omitted.

6

In  a  case,  Banks  v.  The  State  (124  Ga.,  15),  the

court  had  said  of a  similar  provision:

On  the  face  of it the  purpose  of the  act  is to
punish  fraudulent  practices,  not  the  mere  fail-
ure  to  pay  a debt.  Thus  considered,  it is con-
stitutional;  otherwise  it would  not  be  so.
In  McIntosh  v.  The  State  (117  Ala.,  128,  130),  the

court  said:

Refunding  the  money,  or  restoring  other
property,  with  which  the  employer,  by reason
of the contract,  was  induced  to part, is a princi-
pal  purpose  the  statute  is intended  to  enforce.
When  the  money  is refunded,  or  the  property
restored,  the  employer  is  saved  from  injury
or  loss,  and  subsequent  breaches  of  the  con-
tract  must  be  redressed  by the  pursuit  of civil
The  rendition  of service  by the  de-
remedies.
fendant  for  a  period  more  than  sufficient  to
have  repaid  the  money  advanced  to  him
takes  away  an  essential  ingredient  of  the
offense.

These  cases  show  the  relation  of  this  statute  to
the  state  constitution.  In  none  of  them  was  the
question  of  repugnancy  to  the  United  States  Con-
stitution  urged.  In  the  case  at  bar,  however,  the
statute  was  attacked  upon  the  ground  that  it  vio-
lated  the  thirteenth  amendment  in  that  its  effect  is
to  subject  the  defendant  to  involuntary  servitude

While  it is clear  that  a  mere  breach  of con-
tract  can  not  be  made  the  foundation  for  a
criminal  offense  and  that  undue  restrictions
can  not  be  placed  on  the  right  of an  individual
to enter  into  contracts,  yet when  the  individual
enters  into  a  contract  with  the  intention  to
perpetrate  a  fraud  it  is  equally  obvious  that
he  passes  over  the  constitutional  boundary
line  in  respect  to  the  free  right  to  contract;
and  it  is  within  legislative  competency  to
enact  a  law  penalizing  the  entering  into  a  con-
tract  with  such  intent  and  obtaining  money
or  other  personal  property  through  such
agency.

This  is  all  that  is effectuated  by the  legisla-
tion  in  question.  On  its  face,  the  purpose  is
to  punish  fraudulent  practices,  not  the  mere
failure  to  pay  a  debt.  Thus  considered,  it  is
(Rece.,  p.  8.)

In  South  Carolina  a  statute,  providing  ‘‘that  any
laborer  working  on  shares  of  crop  or  for  wages  in
money  or  other  valuable  consideration,  under  a
verbal  or  written  contract  to  labor  on  farm  land,  who
shall  receive  advances  either  in money  or  supplies  and
thereafter  willfully  and  without  just  cause  fail  to
perform  the  reasonable  service  required  of him  by the
terms  of  the  said  contract,  shall  be  liable  to  prosecu-
tion  for  a  misdemeanor,”  and  on  conviction  may  be
punished  by  imprisonment  or  fine,  was  before  the
United  States  District  Court  (Ex  parte  Drayton,  153
F.-R.),  and  the  State  Supreme  Court  (Ex  parte  Holl-
man,  608.  E. Rep.,  19).
In both  courts  the statute  was
declared  unconstitutional,  among  other  reasons  as
violating  the  thirteenth  amendment.
In  the  case
in  the  United  States  District  Court  the  Alabama
satute  was  distinguished  from  the  South  Carolina
statute,  although,  it  not  being  in  issue,  no  opinion
was  profiered  as  to  its  constitutionality.

THE  STATUTE  IS  UNCONSTITUTIONAL  AND  VOID  BE-
CAUSE  IT  VIOLATES  THE  FOURTEENTH  AMENDMENT
TO  THE  CONSTITUTION,  IN  THAT  IT  DEPRIVES  A  PER-
soN  OF  LIBERTY  AND  PROPERTY  wiTHOUT  DUE
Process  oF  Law.

It is submitted  that  the  clause  of the  statute  above
cited,  providing  that  certain  acts  shall  be  prima facie
evidence  of  the  intent  to  injure  or  defraud,  is a viola-
tion  of the  fourteenth  amendment.

In  a  recent.  ease,  decided  at  this  term  of the court,
Twining  v.  State  of New  Jersey,  Mr.  Justice  Moody,
delivering  the  opinion  of the  court,  said:

*

*

consistently  with  the  requirements
*
of due  process,  no  change  in ancient  procedure
can  be  made  which  disregards  those  funda-
to  be  ascertained  from
mental  principles,
time  to  time  by  judicial  action,  which  have
relation  to  process  of law  and  protect  the  citi-
zen  in his  private  right,  and  guard  him  against
the  arbitrary  action  of government.

He  then  proceeds  to  cite  some  expressions  of  this
court  supporting  that  idea:  Bank  v.  Okely,  4 Wheat.,
235,  244  (approved  in Hurtado  v.  California,  110 U.S.,
516,  527;  Leeper  v.  Texas,  139  U.  S.,  462,  468;  Scott
v.  McNeal,  154  U.  8.,  34,  45);  Holden  v.  Hardy,  169
U.  S.,  366,  389;  In  re  Kemmier,  136  U.  S.,  436,  448;
West  v.  Louisiana,  194  U.5.,  258,  263.

In  making  the  application  to  this  case  we  are  to
inquire  what  fundamental  principles  which  protect
the  citizen  in  his  private  rights  have  been  disre-
garded  in  the  statute  under  consideration.

The  general  rule  that  it is within  the  acknowledged
power  of  the  legislature  to  prescribe  rules  of  evidence
in  civil  and  criminal  cases  may  be  admitted.
But
the  rule  is  subject  to  qualifications,  especially  in
Although  it  has  been  said,
criminal  proceedings.
“this  power  has  been  often  exercised  by  the  legisla-
ture,  with  the  sanction  of  the  courts,  so  as  to  change
the  burden  of  proof  or  to  affect  the  question  what
shall  be  deemed  prima  facie  evidence  at  the  trial
before  the  jury”’  (Holmes  v.  Hunt,  122  Mass.,  505-
517),  it  must  always  be  understood  that  the  power
is to  be exercised  with  recognition  of the  fundamental
principles  inhering  in  the  law.

In  an  elaborate  opinion  in  People  v.  Cannon,  139
N.  Y.,  32,  where  the  authorities  were  collated  and
examined,  Peckham,  J. (p. 43), said:

It can  not  be disputed  that  the courts  of this
and  other  States  are  committed  to  the  general
principle  that  even  in  criminal  prosecutions
the  legislature  may  with  some  limitations
enact  that  when  certain  facts  have  been  proved
they  shall  be prima facie evidence  of the  exist-
(See  cases
ence  of the  main  fact  in  question.
cited  in  103  N.  Y.,  148,  supra.)
The  limita-
tions  are  that  the  fact  upon  which  the  pre-
sumption  is to rest  must  have  some  fair relation
to,  or  natural  connection  with,  the  main  fact.
The  inference  of the  existence  of the  main  fact
because  of  the  existence  of  the  fact  actually
proved,  must  not  be  merely  and  purely  arbi-
trary,  or  wholly unreasonable,  unnatural,  or ex-
traordinary,  and the  accused  must  have  in each
case  a  fair  opportunity  to  make  his  defense

tion  as  to  it shall  seem  proper.

this  kind  does  not  take  away  or  impair  the

and  no  tendency  whatever,  by  themselves,  to  prove

“Where  an  act,  in  itself  indifferent,’  said  Lord
Mansfield,  in  Rex  v.  Woodfall  (5 Bur.,  2667),  “if  done
with  a  particular  intent  become  criminal,  the  intent
must  be  proved  and  found;  but  where  the  act  is  in

itself  unlawful,  the  proof  of  justification  or  excuse
lies  in  the  defendant,  and  in  failure  thereof  the  law
implies  a  criminal  intent.”

This  statute  makes  these  acts,  in  themselves  as
consistent  with  innocence  as  with  guilt,  sufficient
[lt  presumes  guilt  as  matter  of law.
evidence  of guilt.
And  in  its  practical  operation  it makes  this  a  conclu-
sive  presumption.

By  the  law  of  Alabama  a  person  on  trial  for  a
criminal  offense  can  testify  for  himself;  but  it  has
been  decided  that  he  can  not  testify  as  to  his  un-
“such  mental
communicated  purpose  or

intent,

status,  when  relevant,  being  a  matter  of  inference  to
be  determined  from  the  circumstances  of  the  case  by

the  jury.””.  (McCormick  v.  Joseph,  77  Ala.,  2386-240.)
How  is the  presumption  of guilt  which  this  statute

impresses  upon  the  accused  to  be  rebutted?

not  even  called  as  a  witness.  Why  call  him?

The  principle  that  there  is a  presumption  of

its  enforcement  lies  at  the  foundation  of  the

This  axiomatic  principle  is substantially  destroyed

a  presumption  “unreasonable  and  unnatural.’”’

Bearing  in  mind  that  the  plaintiff  in  error  was  ar-

question  of  whether  the  action  of  the  Supreme  Court  is
a  final  judgment  or  decree  is  a  suit  within  the  meaning
of  Section  T09  of  the
This  imme-
diate  question  was  first  considered  by  this  Court  in  the
case  of  Holmes  vs,  Tennison,
14  Peters  549.  In  this  case
Holmes  presented  a  petition  to  the  Supreme  Court  of

writ  was  issued  and  on  the  return  to  the  writ  the  court

Holmes  then  sued  out  a

the  State  of  Vermont.  The  writ  of  error  was  dismissed,
the  court  being  equally  divided,  as  to  whether  there  was
drawn  in  question  the  validity  of a  treaty  or  a  statute  or
an  authority  exercised  under  the  United  States.
The
question  of  whether  this  court  had  jurisdiction  of  the
proceeding  under  the  25th  section  of  the  Judicial  Act
of  1789,  now  section  709  of  the  Revised  Statutes,  was
discussed  at  length,  The  opinion  of  Chief  Justice  Taney,
that  such  jurisdiction  existed,  was  concurred  in  by  Jus-
lices  Storey,  M’Lean  and  Wayne,  Justice  Catron  in  a
separate  opinion  concurred  in  this  holding.
Justices
Thompson  and
Baldwin  held  that  the  jurisdiction  did
not  exist  and  Justice  Barbour  did  not  consider  this
point.  Thus  it  will  be  observed  that  five  members  of  the
court  held  that  the  jurisdiction  existed.
We  desire  to
call  attention  particularly  to  the  opinion  of  Justice
Baldwin,  which  contains  the  most  elaborate  discussion
of  the  question  that  ean  be  found.
The  following  ex-
cerpts  are  taken  from  his  opinion :

“It  has  been  demonstrated  at  the  bar  that  the
question  brought  forward  on  a  habeas  corpus  is
always  distinet  from  that  which  is  involved  in  the
cause  itself.”

the  question

“This  being  a  mere  inquiry,  which,  without  decid.
ing  upon  the  guilt,  precedes  the  institution  of  a
prosecution,
to  be  determined  is,
whether  the  accused  shall  be  discharged  or  held  to
trial;  and  if  the  latter,  in  what  place  they  are  to
be  tried,  and  whether  they  shall  be  confined  or  ad-
mitted  to  bail.”

ee  at

4

“It  is  an  exercise  of  appellate  jurisdiction  and
we  are  but  revising  the  effect  of  their  (the  lower
court’s)  process,  under  which  the  prisoner  is  de-
tained.”

process,

“It  lies  only  from  a  final  judgment,  in  a_  civil
action,  ete.,  brought  in  a  Circuit  Court  (of  the
United  States)  by  original
or  removed
there  from  a  State  or  District  Court;  consequently,
it  lies  not  upon  a  proceeding  upon  a  habeas  corpus,
which  is  the  exercise  of  appellate  power  commenc-
ing  on  petition,  affidavit  and  motion  for  the  writ,
and  terminating  by an  order  which  the  court  makes
This  order,  from  its  na-
according  to  its  discretion.
ture  and  effect,  is  not  and  cannot  be  final;  for  it
only  discharges  the  party  from  any  further  con-
finement,  under  the  process  under  which  he  was  ar-
rested,  but  not  from  any  other  process  which  may
be  issued  against  him  under  the  same  indictment.”
“In  following  to  its  consequences,  the  settled
principles  of  this  court,  that  in  issuing  and  acting
upen  a  writ  of  habeas  corpus  under  the  14th  section
it  is  by appellate  power,  it  will  appear  that  the  rea-
sons  for  so  considering  this  power  are  most  con-
elusive  against  the  exercise  of  their  appellate  juris-
diction  over  writs  of  error  to  the  proceedings  of  a
State  or  Circuit  Court,  en  such  a  writ  issued  by
either.”

“Let,  then,  whatever  term,  action,

case,  cause,
suit,  be  given  to  a  writ  of  habeas  corpus  and  the
proceedings  upon  it;  let  the  final  action  of  the  court
upon  it  be  called  a  decision,  an  award,  a  judgment.
or  order,  the  character  or  nature  of  either,  and  the
effect  are  the  same;  nothing  is  revised  but  the  proe-
ess  of  arrest,  and  the  decision  on  which  the  process

this  this  inquiry  can  be  determined  by  one  court

“Any  judgment  rendered  by the  court  affects  only
the  process;  nor  can  it  be  in  any  sense  deemed  a
final  judgment  in  a  suit  on  the  cause  itself,  or  a
final  decision  of  the same  so  as  to  make  it  cogniza-
ble  in  this  court  by  any  appellate  jurisdiction,  on
a  writ  of  error  to  a  circuit  court,  under  the  22nd,
or  a  State  court  under  the  25th  section  of  the
Judiciary  Act.”

“To  sustain  a  writ  of  error  on  a  proceeding  on  a
writ  of  habeas  corpus  under  the  Judiciary  Act,  a
mere  inquiry  must  be  construed  to  mean  a  final
judgment,  a  final  decision;  the  cause  of  commit-
ment  becomes  the  cause  of  action,  or  prosecution,
the  suit,  the  original  suit,  the  cause.’

“It  is  not  enough  that  the term  ‘any  suit’  may
embrace  a  case  of  habeas  corpus;  it  must  be  one
which  in  all  other  respects  admits  of  the  action
prescribed  in  the  Judiciary  Act,  in  all  its  provi-
sions  relative  to  the  appellate  jurisdiction  of  this
court.”

Ifolmes  v.  Jennison,  14  Pet.  620,  et seq.

In  Exparte  Milligan,  4  Wall.  2,  the  majority  of  the
Court  held  that  the  proceeding  for  the  writ  of  habeas
corpus  was  a  cause  within  the  meaning  of  the  Four
teenth  Section  of  the  Judiciary  Act  which  dealt  with
the  question  of  certifying  to  the  Supreme  Court  a
division  among  the  Circuit  Judges.

cisions  of  this  Court  in  which  the  question  was  consid:

In  the  case  of  Yiek  Wo,  vs.  Hopkins,  118  U.  8.  356,

The  question  of  practice  and  the  jurisdiction  of
this  Court  to  review  habeas  corpus  on  writ  of  error  was
not  considered.

From  this  review  of  the  decisions  on  this  question  it
will  be  noted  that  the  proposition  has  not  been  distinctly
decided  and  is yet  open  for  determination  by  this  court.
While  it  appears  that  the  weight  of  authority  may  be  in
favor  of  the  exercise  of  the  jurisdiction,  we  submit  that
the  better  reasening  is  opposed  to  the  exercise  by  this
court  of  such  jurisdiction,  in  habeas  corpus  proceedings
on  writ  of  error.

But  if  Section  709  gives  jurisdiction  to  review  in  this
case,  the  question  of  whether  such  jurisdiction  should  be

to  be  void.
As  said  by  Mr.  Justice  Harlan  in  Ex  parte
Royal,  117  U.  S.  241,  “If  the  local  statute  under  which
Royal  was  indicted  be  repugnant  to  the  constitution,  the
prosecution  against  him  has  nothing  upon  which  to  rest
and  the  entire  proceeding  against  him  is  a  nullity.”
And  further:  “An  unconstitutional  law  is  void  and  is
A  con-
no  law.  The  offense  created  by  it  is  no  crime.

11

vietion  under  it  is  not  merely  erroneous,  but  is  illegal
and  void  and  cannot  be  a  legal  cause  of  imprisonment.”
And  again  in  Ex  parte  Parks,  93  0.  8.  18,  Mr.  Justice
Bradley,  speaking  for  the  court,  said:  “Now  it  has  al-
ways  been  held  that  a  mere  error  in  point  of  law,  com-
mitted  by a  court  ina  case  properly  subject  to  its  cogni-
zane,  can  only  be  reviewed  by  the  ordinary  methods  of
appeal  or  writ  of  error;  but  where  the  proceedings  are
not  only  erroneous,  but  entirely  void,—as  where  the
court  is  without  jurisdiction  of  the  person  or  of  the
‘ause,  and  a  party  is  subjected  to  illegal  imprisonment
in  consequence,—the  Superior  Court,  or  judge  invested
with  the  prerogative  power  of  issuing  a  habeas  corpus,
may  review  the  proceedings  by  that  writ,  and  discharge
from  illegal  imprisonment.”

The  same  conclusion  was  reached  in  Ex  parte  Fred-

rich,  149  U.  S.  169.

Counsel  for  the  plaintiff  in  error  admitted  in  the  State
Stpreme  Court  that  the  original  statute  was  valid,  and
it  is  in  now  contended  by counsel  that  the  original  stat-
ute  was  subject  to  attack.  We  again  call  attention  to
the  expression  of  Judge  Jones  in  the  Peonage  Cases:
“It  (the  original  statute  here  involved)  applies  to  all
classes  of  persons  who  commit  the  designated  offense,
This  stat-

SUPREME  COURT  OF  THE
UNITED  STATES.

OCTOBER  TERM,  1908.

No,  538.

ALONZO  BAILEY,

Plaintiff  in  Error.

VS.

THE  STATE  OF  ALABAMA,

Defendant  in  Error.

IN  ERROR  TO  THE  SUPREME  COURT  OF  THE
STATE  OF  ALABAMA.

Brier  or  ALEXANDER  M,  GARBER,  ATTORNEY  GENERAL,
AND  THOMAS  W.  MARTIN,  ASSISTANT  ATTORNEY
GENERAL,  FOR  THE  DEFENDANT  IN
ERROR.

The  plaintiff  in  error  sued  out  a  writ  of  habeas  cor-
pus  befere  the  Hon.  William  H.  Thomas,  Judge  of  the
City  Court  of  Montgomery,  alleging  that  he  was  impris-

2

oned  by  the  Sheriff  in  the  jail  of  Montgomery  County,
Alabama,  under  a  commitment  issued  by  a  Justice  of
the  Peace,  and  praying  that  upon  the  hearing  of  the
petition  he  might  be  discharged  from  the  custody  of  the
Sheriff.

(R.  p.  1.)

On  the  hearing  of  the  writ  of  habeas  corpus,  it  ap-
peared  that  the  petitioner  had  been  committed  to  jail,
by a  Justice  of  the  Peace,  after  a  preliminary  examina-
tion,  for  the  offense  of  obtaining  fifteen  dollars  under  a
contract  in  writing  with  intent  to  injure  or  defraud  his
employer,  in  violation  of  Section  4730  of  the  Criminal
Code  of  Alabama  of  1896  as  amended  by  the  Act  of  the
Legislature  of  Alabama,  approved  October  Ist,  1903,
(General  Acts  1903,  p.  345)  and  as  last  amended  by  the
Act  of  the  Legislature  of  Alabama  approved  August
15th,  1907,  (General  Acts  1907,  p.  636).

It  was  admitted  on  the  hearing  by  the  petitioner
Bailey  and  the  State  that  but  one  witness,  H.  C.  Borden,
was  exainined  on  the  preliminary  trial  of  Bailey  before
the  Justice  of  Peace,  and  the  testimony  of  that  witness
was  again  offered  on  the  hearing  of  the  habeas  corpus
writ.
No  other  evidence  was  offered  on  such  hearing,
except  the  written  contract,  which  recited  that  in  con-
sideration  of  fifteen  dollars  in  hand  paid  to  Bailey  by
the  Riverside  Company,  on  the  date  of  the  contract,  the
said  Bailey  did  “consent,  contract  and  agree  to  work
and  labor  for  the  Riverside  Company  as  a  farm  hand  on
their  Scott’s  Bend  place  in  Montgomery  County,  Ala-
bama,  from  the  30th  day  of  December,  1907,  to  the  30th
day  of  December,  1908.”  The  record  then  recites:

“The  above  contract  was  introduced  in  evidence
that  Alonzo  Bailey
and  witness  further  testified
had  obtained  from  the  Riverside  Co.  at  the  time  of
entering  into  the  contract  fifteen  dollars;  that  the

3

said  Alonzo  Bailey  had  worked  for  the  said  River-
side  Company  during  the  month  of  January,  1908,
and  two  or  three  days  in  February,  1908,  and  since
that  time  Alonzo  Bailey  had  not  performed  any  ser-
vice  for  the  Riverside  Company,  in  accordance
with  the  said  contract;  that  the  said  Alonzo  Bailey
had  not  refunded  the  said  fifteen  dollars  to  the  said
Riverside  Company  and  was  at  present  indebted  to
the  Riverside  Co.  in  the  sum  of  about  $20.00.  The
same  being  the  $15.00  obtained  by defendant  of  the
employer  Company  at  the  time  of  execution  of  con-
tract  and  advances  made  him  over  and  above  the
value  of  the  labor  he  did  perform  under  the  con-
tract,  and  that  witness  nor  the  Riverside  Co.,  or  its
authorized  agents  had  given  the  petitioner  just
cause  for  quitting  the  said  service  or  failure—such
contract”.

(R.  p.  4.)

Thereupon  Bailey  moved  for  his  discharge  on  nine
grounds  but  the  motion  was  overruled.  On  appeal
the  Supreme  Court  of  Alabama  affirmed  the  action  of
the  trial  Judge,  and  the  petitioner  then  sued  out  the
present  writ  of  error  to  this  Court.

THE  STATUTORY  OFFENSE.

The  law  under  which  this  prosecution  was  begun  had
its  origin  in  the  Act  of  the  General  Assembly  of  Ala-
bama,  approved  Feb,  17,  1885,  (Acts  1884-5,  p.  142),  be-
ing  an  act  to  amend  Section  4370  of  the  Code  of  1876.
That  Section,  4370  of  the  Code  of  1876,  before  the  same
was  amended,  was  in  the  following  language:

“Any  person  who,  by any  false  pretense,  or  token,
and  with  the  intent  to  injure  or  defraud,  obtains

4

from  another  any  money  or  other  personal  prop-
erty,  must,  on  conviction,  be  punished  as  if  he  had
stolen  it.”

The  Act  of  Feb.  17,  1885,  above  referred  to  amended
said  Section  4370  of  the  Code  of  1876  so  as  to  read  as
follows,  the  new  matter  in  italics:

“Any  person  who  by  false  pretense  or  token  and
with  the  intent  to  injure  or  defraud,  obtains  from
another  any  money  or  personal  property  or  any
person  who  has  entered  into  a  written  contract,
with,  at  the  time,  the  intent  to  defraud,  to  do  or  to
perform  any  act  or  service  and  in  consideration
thereof  obtains  from  the  hirer  moncy  or  other  per-
sonal  property,  and  who  abandons  the  service  of
such  hirer  without  just  cause,  without  first  repay-
ing  such  money  or  other  advances,  and  with  the  in-
tent  to  injure  or  defraud,  must,  on  conviction,  be
punished  as  if  he  had  stolen  it.”

In  the  Code  of  1886  Section  4270  of  the  Code  of  1876
as  thus  amended  became  two  sections,  namely,  sections
3811  and  3812,  the  former,  3811,  being  identical  with  the
original  section  4370  of  the  Code  of  1876;  and  the  new
matter  added  by the  Act  of  Feb.  17,  1885,  constituting  a
new  Section,  3812.

Section  3812  of  the  Code  of  1886  now  appears  as  Sec-

tion  4730  of  the  Code  of  1896,

The  Act  of  the  Legislature  approved  October  1, 1903,
(Genera!  Acts  1908,  p.  345)  amended  said  Section
4730,  of  the  Code  of  1896,  so  as  to  read  as  follows,  the
new  matter  being  in  italics:

“Any  person  who  with  intent  to  injure  or  defraud
his  employer,  enters  into  a  contract  in  writing  for

5

the  performance  of  any  act  of  service,  and  thereby
obtains  money  or  other  personal  property  from  such
employer,  and  with  like  intent,  and  without  just
cause,  and  without  refunding  such  money,  or  pay-
ing  for  such  property,  refuses  or  fails  to  perform
such  act  or  service,  must,  on  conviction,  be  punished
as  if  he  had  stolen  it,  nd  the  refusal  or  failure  of
any  person,  who  enters  into  such  contract,  to  per-
form  such  act,  or  service,  or  refund  such  money,  or
pay  for  such  property,  without  just  cause,  shall  be
prima  facie  eridence  of  the  intent  to  injure  or  de-
fraud  his  employer.”

The  Act  of  August  15,  1907,  (General  Acts  1907,  p.
630)  further  amended  said
that  as

This  statute  now  appears  as  section  6845  of  the  Code
of  1907,  but  the  Code  was  adopted  before  the  act  was
passed,  and  is  printed  in  the  Code  for  convenience
merely,

We  have  set  forth  in  detail  the  history  of  this  legisla-
tion  in  order  that  the  questions  raised  may  he  consid-
ered  in  the  light  of  and  with  reference  thereto,

This  case  being  brought  to  this  Court  by  writ  of  error
to  the  Supreme  Court  of  Alabama,  the  jurisdiction  of
this  Court  “is  limited  to  the  question  whether  the  plain-
tiff  in  error  has  been  denied  a  right  in  violation  of  the
Constitution,  laws,  or  treaties  of  the  United  States.  The
question  whether  his  imprisonment  is  illegal,  under  the
Constitution  and  laws  of  the  State  is not  open  to  us.”

Yick  Wo  v.  Hopkins,  118  U.  8.  360;  30  L.  220;
Barbier  vy,  Connolly,  113  U.  8.  27;  28  L,  923.

The  plaintiff  in  error  now  insists  upon  six  grounds  of
defense  (R.  ps.  12  and  138)  and  this  Court  is  confined
to  a  consideration  of  the  questions  raised  thereby,

The  first  and  second  grounds  urged  by  plaintiff  in
error  raise  ques  ions  of  fact  which  are  disposed  of,  if
the  prin  facie  presumption,  which  follows  from  the
proof  of  certain  facts,  is  sustained,

The  third  ground  of  defense  assails  the  Act  of  August
15,  1907,  for  that  it  is  alleged  to  deprive  plaintiff  in
error  Of  his  liberiy  without  due  process  of  law  in  viola-
tion  of  section  six  of  the  Constitution  of  the  State  of
Alaama.
But  as  before  pointed  out  the  question  of
whether  the  Act  is  violative  of  the  Constitution  of  Ala-
bama  is  not  open  to  this  Court.
In  the  case  at  bar,  as
well  as  other  cases,  that  court  has  expressly  held  the
Act  good  against  every  question  raised  by  the  six
grounds  referred  to  above.

Bailey  v.  State  of  Ala,  (Case  at  bar) ;
State  v.  Vann,  43  So.  (Ala.)  357;
Thomas  v,  State,  144  Ala.  77;
MeIntosh  y.  State,  117  Ala,  128;
Ix  parte  Riley,  94  Ala.  82.

DUE  PROCESS  OF  LAW.

The  fourth  ground  urged  is  that  the  Act  in  question
violates  that  part  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the
Constitution  of  the  United  States  which  provides  that
no  State  shall  deprive  any  person  of  life,  liberty  or
property  without  due  process  of  law,

This  Court  has  many  times  defined  the  meaning  of  the
In  Dent  v.  West  Virginia,

terms  “due  process  of  law.”
129  U.S.  128,  82  L,  623,  Mr.  Justice  Field  said:

“They  (the  terms,  due  process  of  law)  come  to  us
from  the  law  of  England,  from  which  country  our
jurisprudence  is  to  a  great  extent  derived,  and
their  requirement  was  there  designed  to  secure  the

S

subject  against  the  arbitrary  action  of  the  Crown
amd  place  him  under  the  pretection  of  the  law,  They
were  deemed  to  be  equivalent  to  ‘the  law
of  the
land.”
In  this  country,  the  requirement  is  jutended
fo  have  a  similar  effect  against  legislative  power,
that  is,  to  secure  the  citizen  against  any  arbitrary
deprivation  of  his  rights,  whether  relating  to  his
life,  his  liberty,  or  his  property.
Legislation  must
necessarily  vary  with  the  different
objects  upon
Which  it  is  designed  to  operate.
It  is  sufficient.  for
the  purposes  of  this  case,  to  say  that  legislation  is
het  open  to  the  charge  of  depriving  one  of  his
rights  without  due  process  of  law,  if  it  be  general
in  its  operation  upon  the  subjects  to  which  it  re-
lates  and  is  enforceable  in  the  usual  modes  estab-
lished  in  the  administration  of  government  with  re-
speocl  to  kindred  matters,  that  is.  by  process  or  pro-
COCUTIYS  adapted  to  the  nature  of  the  case,
The
great  purpose  of  the  requirement
to  exclude
everything  that  is  arbitrary  and  capricious  in  lewis-
lation  affecting  the  rights  of  the  citizen.”

is

And  in  Leeper  vy,  Texas,  120  0S.  462.85  L.  2:85.  Mr.
Chief  Justice  Fuller  said  with  reference  to  thy  power  of
the  State  in  dealing  with  erime:

nd  impartial  justice  under  the  law:  that  law  in  its
regular  course  of  administration  through  courts  of
justice  is  due  process,  and  when  secured  by  the  law
of  the  State,  the  constitutional  requirement  is  sat-
isfied;  and  that  due  process  is  so  secured  by  laws

9

operating  on  ail  alike,  and  not  subjecting  the  indi-
ridual  to  the  arbitrary  evrercise  of  the  powers  of
government  unrestraincd  by  the  established  prin-
ciples  of  private  right  and  distributive  justice.”

In  order  for  the  State  to  obtain  the  benetit:

of  the
prima  facie  presumption  under  the  Act  in  question,  it
will  be  necessary  to  prove  on  the  final  trial  of  the  plain-
tiff  in  error  that  he  entered  into  a  contract  in  writing
to  perform  service  for  the  Riverside  Company  and  there’
hy  obtained  money  from  such  Company  and  that  with-
out  just  cause  and  without  refunding  the  money  he  re-
fused  or  failed  to  perform  the  service,
Every  fact  whieh
enters  jnto  the  offense,  except  the  intent  to  injure  or
defraud,  must  be  proven  before  this  presumption  ob-
tains,  And  the  question  is  thus  presented  whether  this
statute,  which  declares  such  a  rule  of  evidence,  deprives
the  plaintiff  in  error  of  life,  liberty  or  property  without
due  process  of  law.

The  validity  of  this  provision  of  the  statute  has  been
considered  and  upheld  in  three  different  cases  by  the
Supreme  Court  of  Alabama:

Bailey  v.  State,  (at  bar) ;
State  v.  Vann,  48  South,  ( Ala.)  357;
State  v.  Thomas,  144  Ala.  77.

simply  declares  the  intent,  declares  a  prima  facie

In  Fong  v,  United  States,  149  U.S.  697,  729,  supra,
thi  sCourt  considered  the  Act  of  May  Sth,  1892,  being

The  Act  provided  that  Chinese  laborers
United  States.
then  in  the  United  States  and  who  were  entitled  to  re-

States  after  the  time  named  without  such  certificate,  he
should  be  deemed  and  adjudged  to  be  unlawfully  within
the  United  States,  unless  he  should  establish  clearly  to
the  satisfaction  of the  judge  “that  by  reason  of  accident,

to  procure  his  certificate,  and  to  the  satisfaction  of  said

This  neither  conclusively  determines
the  guilt  or  innocence  of  the  party  who  is  accused,

makes.

prima  facie  presumption  which  the  Legislature  sought
to  give  to  such  act  of  delivery.

In  State  v,  Beach,  147  Ind.  74,  supra,  the  court  con-
sidered  a  statute  which  provided  that  the  failure  of  a
banker  within  thirty  days  after  receiving  a  deposit,
should  be  prima  facie  evidence  on  the  part  of  the  banker
of  an  intent  to  defraud  at  the  time  of  receiving  the  de
posit.  The  court  said,  at  p.  83:

“The  statute  enables  the  State  to  make  prima
fagie  proof  of  the  intent  to  defraud  by  showing  the
failure,  suspension  or  involuntary  liquidation  of
the  banker,  broker,  banking  company  or  incorpor-
ated  bank,  as  the  ease  may  be,  within  thirty  days
after  the  deposit  was  received.”

In  prescribing  the  present  rule  of  evidence,
the  Legislature  has  left  a  full  and  fair  opportunity  to
the  defendant  to  make  his  defense  and  submit  all  the
facts  to  the  jury  to  be  weighed  by  them.

As  is  well  pointed  out  in  24  Cye,  192,  a  prima  facie
rule  of  evidence  in  a  criminal  case  “does  not  overcome
the  presumption  of  innocence  or  change  the  burden  of
proof  or  require  the  jury  to  convict,  uniess  they  are  sat-
isfied  from  all  the  evidence  of  the  guilt  of  the  accused
beyond  a  reasonable  doubt.”  The  present  statute  comes
directly  within  this  principle  of  law  on  which  reliance
is  placed  by  plaintiff  in  error,

facts  of  the  offense  merely  calls  upon  the  defendant  to

As  said  by  Judge  Brickell,  in
session  of  stolen  goods,
Martin  +.  State,  104  Ala.  78,  “It  is  the  settled  law  of  this
State,  that  the  recent  possession  of  stolen  goods,  im-
poses  on  the  possessor  the  onus  of  explaining  the  pos-

At  the  same  time,  our  Court  recognizes  the  doctrine
See  Newsom  v.  State,

of  the  presumption  of  innocence.
107  Ala,  133,  139,

A  rule  of  evidence,  whether  established  by  statute  or
decisions,  is  entirely  consistent  with  that  presumption.
The  refusal  or  failure  to  perform  the  act  or  service  is
by the  statute  made  prima  facie  evidence  of  the  intent  to
injure  or  defraud  the  employer.
The  question  may  be
suggested,  what  intent  to  defraud  is  here  referred  to?
The  answer  will  be  found  in  the  opening  clause  of  the
statute,  namely,  the  intent  to  defraud  at  the  time  of  en-
tering  into  the  contract;  and  the  further  and  like  intent
which  must  exist  at  the  time  of  the  refusal  of failure  to
perform.
The  offense  would  have  been  complete  with-
out  any  reference  to  the  intent  that  might  exist  at  the
time  of  the  failure  or  refusal  to  perform.
The  Legisla-
ture  has  gone  further  than  was  pecessary  in  declaring
the  offense.

EQUAL  PROTECTION  OF  THE  LAWS.

In  the  case  of  Moore  v.  Missouri,  159  U.S.  673,  40  L.
301,  Mr.  Chief  Justice  Fuller  said  with  respect  to  this
question  :

“The  14th  Amendment  means  ‘that  no  person  or
class  of  persons  shall  be  denied  the  same  protection

other  classes  in  the  same  place  and  under  like  e'r-
cumstances’
doctrine  — is
that  that  amendment,  in  respect  to  the  adminisira-
tion  of  criminal  justice,  requires  that  no  different
decree  or  higher  punishment  shall  be  imposed  on
one  than  is  imposed  on  all  for  like  offenses”.

It  is  contended  that  the  act  in  question  violates  the
Fourteenth  Amendment  in  the  respect  under  considera
tion  in  that  it  applies  only  te  persons  who  enter  inie
contracts  in  writing  fer  the  performance  of  an  act  or
service  or  for  the  rent  of  lands,  that  it  does  not  bear
equally  upon  the  employee  and  employer  and  that  ‘t  is
applied  only  against  farm  laborers  and  the  colored  race.
As  we  pointed  out  before,  the  statute  had  its  origin  as
an  amendment  to  the  statute  punishing  the  obtaining  of
money  or  other  personal  property  by  false  pretenses.
Every  person  within  the  State  of  Alabama  Is  subject  to
punishment  for  obtaining  money  by  false  pretense  and
it  is  not  contended  that  that  statute  denies  to  any  one
the  equal  protection  of  the  laws.

The  present  statute,  on  its  face,  likewise  applies  to
every  person  who  with  a  fraudulent  purpose  enters  into
a  written  contract  to  perform  an  act  or  service  for  an-
other,  and  thereby  obtains  money,

So  it  is  well  settled  that  the  Legislature  may  classify

Class  mentioned;  and  of  this  there  is  no  pretense’.

And  see  to  the  same  effect  Tinsley  vo  Anderson,  171

U.  S.  101,  43  L,  91.

The  present  contention  is  wholly  without  merit.  Tis
Court  will  examine  the  record  in  this  case  in  vain  for
any  suggestion  that  the  plaintif—  in  error  is  a  mersber
of the  colored  race,
For  aught  that  appears,  Bailey  may
hea  white  man,  And  in  this  condition  of  the  record  the
contention  may  be  passed  without
further  discussion.

than  as  presented,  we  answer  the  contention  with  the
rule  Jaid  down  by  Mr.  Justice  Field  in  soon  Hfing  vy.
Crowley,  supra,  that  there  was  no  pretense  in  the  court
below,  that  in  its  enforcement  the  law  was  made  to  op-
erate  only  against  farm  laborers  and  the  negro  race,  This
Court  cannot  act  on  a  suggestion  of  counsel  nor  will  it

INVOLUNTARY  SERVITUDE.

a ei ard

Neither  the  original  nor  the  amended  statute  punishes
the  plaiatiif  in  error  for  debt.
The  13th  Amendment
provides  that  “Neither  slavery  nor  involuntary  servi-
tude,  except  as  a@  punishment  for  crime  whereof  the
party  shall  have  been  duly  convieted,  shall  exist  within
the  United  Stats,  or  any  place  subject  to  their  juris-

diction.”

In  the  brief  of  counsel  for  appellant  filed  in  the  State
Supreme  Court,  it  was  admitted  that  “the  statute  here
in  questioned  was  a  valid  statute  prior  to  the  amend-
ment  of  1903",  And  indeed  the  admission  was  entirely
correct.
But  as  the  proposition  which  we  are  now  con-
sidering  may  involve  the  original  statute,  we  will  briefly
touch  upon  its  validity.

If  the  statute  now  before  the  Court  provides  a  pun-
ishment  for  crime,  it  is  a  valid  enactment  so  far  as  the
13th  Amendment  is  concerned,

In  Ex  Parte  Riley,  94  Ala.  82,  after  quoting  the  stat-

ute,  the  Court  said:

“The  effect  of  this  statute  is  to  provide  for  the
punishment  criminally  of  a  certain  class  of  frauds
Which  are  perpetuated  by  means  of  promises  not
meant  to  be  kept.
These  frauds  closely  resemble
those  perpetrated  by  means  of  false  pretenses  or
tokens,  but  are  not  punishable  under  the  statute  on
that  subject,  as  a  false  pretense  is  a  false  repre-
sentation  relating  to  some  existing  or  past  fact,  and
does  not  include  a  promise  of  something  to  be  done
A  mere  breach  of  a  con-
*
in  the  future.
tract  is not  by the  statute  made  a  crime.
The  erim-
inal  feature  of  the  transaction  is  wanting  unless
the  accused  encered  into  the  contract  with  intent

*

*

32

to  injure  or  defraud  his  employer,  and  unless  his
refusal  to  perform  was  with  like  intent  and  with-
out  just  cause.”

Again  in  State  vs.  Vann,  43  Sou.  ( Ala.)  557,  the  Court
pointed  out  that  “Under  the  former  construction  of  the
statute  by  this  Court,  while  the  essence  of  the  crime  is  a
fraudulent  intent,  the  statute  is  intended
to  prevent
fraud  and  not  to  imprison  for  debt.”

And  in  the  case  at  bar  Judge  Denson,  after  reviewing

the  cases,  held  that:

“It  is  within  legislative  competency  to  enact  a
law  penalizing  the  entering  into  a  contract,  with
such  intent,  (to  perpetrate  a  fraud)  and  obtaining
money  or  other  personal  property  through  such
agency.

“This  is  all  that  is  effectuated  by  the  legislation
On  its  face  the  purpose  is  to  punish
in  question,
fraudulent  practices,  not  the  mere  failure  to  pay  a
Thus  considered,  it  is  constitutional.”
debt.

Without  further  discussing  this  question  we  think  it
clear  that  the  original  statute  was  a  valid  enactment,
for  that  its  purpose  was  to  punish  fraud.

And  we  are  unable  to  see  how  the  amendment  to  the
statute  has  changed  its  purpose  in  that  respect.
The
rule  of  evidence  as  it  now  exists  does  not  take  away  the
fraud  which  must  be  involved  in  such  case.
The  jury  is
at  all  times  at  liberty  to  find
a  defendant  not  guilty
even  though  he  fails  to  testify.

The  statute  we  are  now  considering  is  wholly  differ-
ent  from  the  law  held  to  be  obnoxious  to  the  State  con-
stitution,  in  Toney  vy,  State,  141  Ala.  120;  and  in  the
Peonage  Cases,  123  Fed.  671.  As  said  by Judge  Denson

Mr,  Ball  in  his  brief  for  plaintiff  in  error  sets  forth
what  he  terms  six  practical  applications  of  the  statute.
In  each  instance  he  presents  the  cause  for  quitting  as

facie  evidence  of  the  fraudulent  intent.
The  fallacy  of

stance  mentioned  by  him  the  excuse  which  the  employee
may  have  for  quitting  the  service  would  constitute  a
good  cause  therefor,  if  not  as  a  matter  of  law  to  be  so
held  by  the  court,  certainly  as  matter  of  fact  which  any
jury  of  intelligent  men  would  sustain.  The  present  plain-
tiff  in  error  does  not  bring  himself  within  any  of  the
illustrations  so  used  by  counsel,
They  each  constitute

Almost  any  criminal  statute  can  by this  ingenious  argu-
